<p>The Fujita scale and the Enhanced Fujita Scale rate tornadoes by damage caused. The Enhanced Fujita (EF) Scale was an update to the older Fujita scale, by <a href="/wiki/Expert_elicitation" title="Expert elicitation">expert elicitation</a>, using engineered wind estimates and better damage descriptions. The EF Scale was designed so that a tornado rated on the Fujita scale would receive the same numerical rating, and was implemented starting in the United States in 2007. An EF0 tornado will probably damage trees but not substantial structures, whereas an <a href="/wiki/List_of_F5_and_EF5_tornadoes" title="List of F5 and EF5 tornadoes">EF5</a> tornado can rip buildings off their foundations leaving them bare and even deform large <a href="/wiki/Skyscraper" title="Skyscraper">skyscrapers</a>. The similar TORRO scale ranges from a T0 for extremely weak tornadoes to T11 for the most powerful known tornadoes. <a href="/wiki/Pulse-Doppler_radar" title="Pulse-Doppler radar">Doppler</a> <a href="/wiki/Weather_radar" title="Weather radar">weather radar</a> data, <a href="/wiki/Photogrammetry" title="Photogrammetry">photogrammetry</a>, and ground swirl patterns (<a href="/wiki/Cycloid" title="Cycloid">cycloidal</a> marks) may also be analyzed to determine intensity and award a rating.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SPC_FAQ_5-9"><a href="#cite_note-SPC_FAQ-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Shown_here_May_22,_2013,_is_an_aerial_view_of_homes_destroyed_by_a_tornado_in_Moore,_Okla_130522-F-IE715-379.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2472" data-file-width="4016" decoding="async" height="135" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Shown_here_May_22%2C_2013%2C_is_an_aerial_view_of_homes_destroyed_by_a_tornado_in_Moore%2C_Okla_130522-F-IE715-379.jpg/220px-Shown_here_May_22%2C_2013%2C_is_an_aerial_view_of_homes_destroyed_by_a_tornado_in_Moore%2C_Okla_130522-F-IE715-379.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Shown_here_May_22%2C_2013%2C_is_an_aerial_view_of_homes_destroyed_by_a_tornado_in_Moore%2C_Okla_130522-F-IE715-379.jpg/330px-Shown_here_May_22%2C_2013%2C_is_an_aerial_view_of_homes_destroyed_by_a_tornado_in_Moore%2C_Okla_130522-F-IE715-379.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Shown_here_May_22%2C_2013%2C_is_an_aerial_view_of_homes_destroyed_by_a_tornado_in_Moore%2C_Okla_130522-F-IE715-379.jpg/440px-Shown_here_May_22%2C_2013%2C_is_an_aerial_view_of_homes_destroyed_by_a_tornado_in_Moore%2C_Okla_130522-F-IE715-379.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Shown_here_May_22,_2013,_is_an_aerial_view_of_homes_destroyed_by_a_tornado_in_Moore,_Okla_130522-F-IE715-379.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>On May 20, 2013, a <a href="/wiki/2013_Moore_tornado" title="2013 Moore tornado">large tornado</a> of the highest category, EF5, ravaged <a href="/wiki/Moore,_Oklahoma" title="Moore, Oklahoma">Moore, Oklahoma</a></div></div></div>
<p>Tornadoes vary in intensity regardless of shape, size, and location, though strong tornadoes are typically larger than weak tornadoes. The association with track length and duration also varies, although longer track tornadoes tend to be stronger.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-width/length_intensity_relationship_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-width/length_intensity_relationship-69">[69]</a></sup> In the case of violent tornadoes, only a small portion of the path is of violent intensity, most of the higher intensity from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Multiple_vortex_tornado" title="Multiple vortex tornado">subvortices</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-significant_tornadoes_23-6"><a href="#cite_note-significant_tornadoes-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the United States, 80% of tornadoes are EF0 and EF1 (T0 through T3) tornadoes. The rate of occurrence drops off quickly with increasing strength—less than 1% are violent tornadoes (EF4, T8 or stronger).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Basic_Spotter_Guide_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-Basic_Spotter_Guide-70">[70]</a></sup> Current records may significantly underestimate the frequency of strong (EF2-EF3) and violent (EF4-EF5) tornadoes, as damage-based intensity estimates are limited to structures and vegetation that a tornado impacts. A tornado may be much stronger than its damage-based rating indicates if its strongest winds occur away from suitable damage indicators, such as in an open field.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EFunderstimate_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-EFunderstimate-72">[72]</a></sup> Outside <a href="/wiki/Tornado_Alley" title="Tornado Alley">Tornado Alley</a>, and North America in general, violent tornadoes are extremely rare. This is apparently mostly due to the lesser number of tornadoes overall, as research shows that tornado intensity distributions are fairly similar worldwide. A few significant tornadoes occur annually in Europe, Asia, southern Africa, and southeastern South America.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-intensity_distribution_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-intensity_distribution-73">[73]</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Climatology">Climatology</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Tornado_climatology" title="Tornado climatology">Tornado climatology</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Globdisttornado.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1465" data-file-width="2729" decoding="async" height="118" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Globdisttornado.jpg/220px-Globdisttornado.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Globdisttornado.jpg/330px-Globdisttornado.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Globdisttornado.jpg/440px-Globdisttornado.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Globdisttornado.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Areas worldwide where tornadoes are most likely, indicated by orange shading</div></div></div>
<p>The United States has the most tornadoes of any country, nearly four times more than estimated in all of Europe, excluding waterspouts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-European_tornado_climatology_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-European_tornado_climatology-74">[74]</a></sup> This is mostly due to the unique geography of the continent. North America is a large continent that extends from the <a href="/wiki/Tropics" title="Tropics">tropics</a> north into <a href="/wiki/Arctic" title="Arctic">arctic</a> areas, and has no major east–west mountain range to block air flow between these two areas. In the <a href="/wiki/Middle_latitudes" title="Middle latitudes">middle latitudes</a>, where most tornadoes of the world occur, the <a href="/wiki/Rocky_Mountains" title="Rocky Mountains">Rocky Mountains</a> block moisture and buckle the <a href="/wiki/Prevailing_winds" title="Prevailing winds">atmospheric flow</a>, forcing drier air at mid-levels of the <a href="/wiki/Troposphere" title="Troposphere">troposphere</a> due to downsloped winds, and causing <a href="/wiki/Cyclogenesis" title="Cyclogenesis">the formation of a low pressure area</a> downwind to the east of the mountains. Increased westerly flow off the Rockies force the formation of a <a href="/wiki/Dry_line" title="Dry line">dry line</a> when the flow aloft is strong,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> while the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Mexico" title="Gulf of Mexico">Gulf of Mexico</a> fuels abundant low-level moisture in the southerly flow to its east. This unique topography allows for frequent collisions of warm and cold air, the conditions that breed strong, long-lived storms throughout the year. A large portion of these tornadoes form in an area of the <a href="/wiki/Central_United_States" title="Central United States">central United States</a> known as <a href="/wiki/Tornado_Alley" title="Tornado Alley">Tornado Alley</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Science_News_1_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-Science_News_1-76">[76]</a></sup> This area extends into Canada, particularly <a href="/wiki/Ontario" title="Ontario">Ontario</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prairie_Provinces" title="Prairie Provinces">Prairie Provinces</a>, although southeast <a href="/wiki/Quebec" title="Quebec">Quebec</a>, the interior of <a href="/wiki/British_Columbia" title="British Columbia">British Columbia</a>, and western <a href="/wiki/New_Brunswick" title="New Brunswick">New Brunswick</a> are also tornado-prone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> Tornadoes also occur across northeastern Mexico.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SPC_FAQ_5-10"><a href="#cite_note-SPC_FAQ-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p><p>The United States averages about 1,200 tornadoes per year, followed by Canada, averaging 62 reported per year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Canada62_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-Canada62-78">[78]</a></sup> NOAA's has a higher average 100 per year in Canada.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Canada100_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-Canada100-79">[79]</a></sup> The Netherlands has the highest average number of recorded tornadoes per area of any country (more than 20, or 0.00048/km<sup>2</sup>, 0.0012/sq mi annually), followed by the UK (around 33, 0.00013/km<sup>2</sup>, 0.00034/sq mi per year), although those are of lower intensity, briefer<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> and cause minor damage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-European_tornado_climatology_74-1"><a href="#cite_note-European_tornado_climatology-74">[74]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tornado_Alley.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="582" data-file-width="742" decoding="async" height="173" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Tornado_Alley.gif/220px-Tornado_Alley.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Tornado_Alley.gif/330px-Tornado_Alley.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Tornado_Alley.gif/440px-Tornado_Alley.gif 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tornado_Alley.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Intense tornado activity in the United States. The darker-colored areas denote the area commonly referred to as <a href="/wiki/Tornado_Alley" title="Tornado Alley">Tornado Alley</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Tornadoes kill an average of 179 people per year in Bangladesh, the most in the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bangladesh_tornado_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bangladesh_tornado-82">[82]</a></sup> Reasons for this include the region's high population density, poor construction quality, and lack of tornado safety knowledge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bangladesh_tornado_82-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bangladesh_tornado-82">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> Other areas of the world that have frequent tornadoes include South Africa, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/La_Plata_Basin" title="La Plata Basin">La Plata Basin</a> area, portions of Europe, Australia and New Zealand, and far eastern Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EB_tornado_climatology_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-EB_tornado_climatology-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup>
</p><p>Tornadoes are most common in spring and least common in winter, but tornadoes can occur any time of year that favorable conditions occur.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-significant_tornadoes_23-7"><a href="#cite_note-significant_tornadoes-23">[23]</a></sup> Spring and fall experience peaks of activity as those are the seasons when stronger winds, wind shear, and atmospheric instability are present.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LouieLouie_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-LouieLouie-85">[85]</a></sup> Tornadoes are focused in the right front quadrant of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Landfall_(meteorology)" title="Landfall (meteorology)">landfalling</a> tropical cyclones, which tend to occur in the late summer and autumn. Tornadoes can also be spawned as a result of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eyewall_mesovortices" title="Eyewall mesovortices">eyewall mesovortices</a>, which persist until landfall.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AOML_FAQ_L6_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-AOML_FAQ_L6-86">[86]</a></sup>
</p><p>Tornado occurrence is highly dependent on the time of day, because of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_radiation" title="Solar radiation">solar heating</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tornado_time_of_day_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-tornado_time_of_day-87">[87]</a></sup> Worldwide, most tornadoes occur in the late afternoon, between 15:00 (3 pm) and 19:00 (7 pm) local time, with a peak near 17:00 (5 pm).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> Destructive tornadoes can occur at any time of day. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tupelo-Gainesville_tornado_outbreak" title="Tupelo-Gainesville tornado outbreak">Gainesville Tornado</a> of 1936, one of the deadliest tornadoes in history, occurred at 8:30 am local time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-significant_tornadoes_23-8"><a href="#cite_note-significant_tornadoes-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p><p>The United Kingdom has the highest incidence of tornadoes per unit area of land in the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> Unsettled conditions and weather fronts transverse the British Isles at all times of the years, and are responsible for spawning the tornadoes, which consequently form at all times of the year. The United Kingdom has at least 34 tornadoes per year and possibly as many as 50.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> Most tornadoes in the United Kingdom are weak, but they are occasionally destructive. For example, the Birmingham tornado of 2005 and the London tornado of 2006 both registered F2 on the Fujita scale and both caused significant damage and injury.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Associations_with_climate_and_climate_change">Associations with climate and climate change</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tornado_US_annual_count.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="973" data-file-width="1532" decoding="async" height="140" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Tornado_US_annual_count.svg/220px-Tornado_US_annual_count.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Tornado_US_annual_count.svg/330px-Tornado_US_annual_count.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Tornado_US_annual_count.svg/440px-Tornado_US_annual_count.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tornado_US_annual_count.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>U. S. annual count of confirmed tornadoes. The count uptick in 1990 is coincident with the introduction of doppler weather radar.</div></div></div>
<p>Associations with various <a href="/wiki/Climate" title="Climate">climate</a> and environmental trends exist. For example, an increase in the <a href="/wiki/Sea_surface_temperature" title="Sea surface temperature">sea surface temperature</a> of a source region (e.g. Gulf of Mexico and <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">Mediterranean Sea</a>) increases atmospheric moisture content. Increased moisture can fuel an increase in <a href="/wiki/Severe_weather" title="Severe weather">severe weather</a> and tornado activity, particularly in the cool season.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Edwards_GoM_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-Edwards_GoM-96">[96]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some evidence does suggest that the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Southern_Oscillation" title="Southern Oscillation">Southern Oscillation</a> is weakly correlated with changes in tornado activity, which vary by season and region, as well as whether the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ENSO" title="ENSO">ENSO</a> phase is that of <a href="/wiki/El_Ni%C3%B1o" title="El Niño">El Niño</a> or <a href="/wiki/La_Ni%C3%B1a" title="La Niña">La Niña</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AGU_ENSO_tor_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-AGU_ENSO_tor-97">[97]</a></sup> Research has found that fewer tornadoes and hailstorms occur in winter and spring in the U.S. central and southern plains during El Niño, and more occur during La Niña, than in years when temperatures in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pacific" title="Pacific">Pacific</a> are relatively stable. Ocean conditions could be used to forecast extreme spring storm events several months in advance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup>
</p><p>Climatic shifts may affect tornadoes via <a href="/wiki/Teleconnection" title="Teleconnection">teleconnections</a> in shifting the jet stream and the larger weather patterns. The climate-tornado link is confounded by the forces affecting larger patterns and by the local, nuanced nature of tornadoes. Although it is reasonable to suspect that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">global warming</a> may affect trends in tornado activity,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-agw_tstm_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-agw_tstm-99">[99]</a></sup> any such effect is not yet identifiable due to the complexity, local nature of the storms, and database quality issues. Any effect would vary by region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IPCC4-WGI_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-IPCC4-WGI-100">[100]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Detection">Detection</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tornado1857.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="533" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="115" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Tornado1857.jpg/220px-Tornado1857.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Tornado1857.jpg/330px-Tornado1857.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Tornado1857.jpg/440px-Tornado1857.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tornado1857.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Path of a tornado across Wisconsin on August 21, 1857</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Convective_storm_detection" title="Convective storm detection">Convective storm detection</a></div>
<p>Rigorous attempts to warn of tornadoes began in the United States in the mid-20th century. Before the 1950s, the only method of detecting a tornado was by someone seeing it on the ground. Often, news of a tornado would reach a local weather office after the storm. However, with the advent of weather radar, areas near a local office could get advance warning of severe weather. The first public <a href="/wiki/Tornado_warning" title="Tornado warning">tornado warnings</a> were issued in 1950 and the first <a href="/wiki/Tornado_watch" title="Tornado watch">tornado watches</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Convective_outlooks" title="Convective outlooks">convective outlooks</a> came about in 1952. In 1953, it was confirmed that <a href="/wiki/Hook_echo" title="Hook echo">hook echoes</a> were associated with tornadoes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> By recognizing these radar signatures, meteorologists could detect thunderstorms probably producing tornadoes from several miles away.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hook_echoes_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-hook_echoes-102">[102]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Radar">Radar</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Pulse-Doppler_radar" title="Pulse-Doppler radar">Pulse-Doppler radar</a> and <a href="/wiki/Weather_radar" title="Weather radar">weather radar</a></div>
<p>Today most developed countries have a network of weather radars, which serves as the primary method of detecting hook signatures that are likely associated with tornadoes. In the United States and a few other countries, Doppler weather radar stations are used. These devices measure the velocity and radial <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Direction_(geometry,_geography)" title="Direction (geometry, geography)">direction</a> (towards or away from the radar) of the winds within a storm, and so can spot evidence of rotation in storms from over 160 km (100 miles) away. When storms are distant from a radar, only areas high within the storm are observed and the important areas below are not sampled.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-airbusradar_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-airbusradar-103">[103]</a></sup> Data resolution also decreases with distance from the radar. Some meteorological situations leading to tornadogenesis are not readily detectable by radar and tornado development may occasionally take place more quickly than radar can complete a scan and send the batch of data. Doppler radar systems can detect <a href="/wiki/Mesocyclone" title="Mesocyclone">mesocyclones</a> within a supercell thunderstorm. This allows meteorologists to predict tornado formations throughout thunderstorms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="center"><div class="thumb tnone"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:702px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:05june-rapiddow-wide.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="469" data-file-width="1081" decoding="async" height="304" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/05june-rapiddow-wide.gif/700px-05june-rapiddow-wide.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/05june-rapiddow-wide.gif/1050px-05june-rapiddow-wide.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/a8/05june-rapiddow-wide.gif 2x" width="700"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:05june-rapiddow-wide.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Doppler_on_Wheels" title="Doppler on Wheels">Doppler on Wheels</a> radar loop of a <a href="/wiki/Hook_echo" title="Hook echo">hook echo</a> and associated mesocyclone in <a href="/wiki/Tornadoes_of_2009#June_5" title="Tornadoes of 2009">Goshen County, Wyoming on June 5, 2009</a>. Strong mesocyclones show up as adjacent areas of yellow and blue (on other radars, bright red and bright green), and usually indicate an imminent or occurring tornado.</div></div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Storm_spotting">Storm spotting</span></h3>
<p><span class="citation-needed-content" style="padding-left:0.1em; padding-right:0.1em; color:#595959; border:1px solid #DDD;">In the mid-1970s, the U.S. <a href="/wiki/National_Weather_Service" title="National Weather Service">National Weather Service</a> (NWS) increased its efforts to train <a href="/wiki/Storm_spotting" title="Storm spotting">storm spotters</a> so they could spot key features of storms that indicate severe hail, damaging winds, and tornadoes, as well as storm damage and <a href="/wiki/Flash_flood" title="Flash flood">flash flooding</a>. The program was called <b><a href="/wiki/Skywarn" title="Skywarn">Skywarn</a></b>, and the spotters were local sheriff's deputies, state troopers, firefighters, ambulance drivers, <a href="/wiki/Amateur_radio_operator" title="Amateur radio operator">amateur radio operators</a>, <a href="/wiki/Civil_defense" title="Civil defense">civil defense</a> (now <a href="/wiki/Emergency_management" title="Emergency management">emergency management</a>) spotters, <a href="/wiki/Storm_chasing" title="Storm chasing">storm chasers</a>, and ordinary citizens. When severe weather is anticipated, local weather service offices request these spotters to look out for severe weather and report any tornadoes immediately, so that the office can warn of the hazard.</span><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Spotters usually are trained by the NWS on behalf of their respective organizations, and report to them. The organizations activate public warning systems such as <a href="/wiki/Civil_defense_siren" title="Civil defense siren">sirens</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Emergency_Alert_System" title="Emergency Alert System">Emergency Alert System</a> (EAS), and they forward the report to the NWS.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spotter_history_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-spotter_history-105">[105]</a></sup>
There are more than 230,000 trained Skywarn weather spotters across the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NWS_SKYWARN_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-NWS_SKYWARN-106">[106]</a></sup>
</p><p>In Canada, a similar network of volunteer weather watchers, called <b><a href="/wiki/Canwarn" title="Canwarn">Canwarn</a></b>, helps spot severe weather, with more than 1,000 volunteers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-environment_Canada_detection_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-environment_Canada_detection-107">[107]</a></sup> In Europe, several nations are organizing spotter networks under the auspices of <a href="/wiki/Skywarn_Europe" title="Skywarn Europe">Skywarn Europe</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> and the Tornado and Storm Research Organisation (TORRO) has maintained a network of spotters in the United Kingdom since 1974.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup>
</p><p>Storm spotters are required because radar systems such as <a href="/wiki/NEXRAD" title="NEXRAD">NEXRAD</a>  detect signatures that suggest the presence of tornadoes, rather than tornadoes as such.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NSSLsearch_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-NSSLsearch-110">[110]</a></sup> Radar may give a warning before there is any visual evidence of a tornado or an imminent one, but <a href="/wiki/Ground_truth" title="Ground truth">ground truth</a> from an observer can give definitive information.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> The spotter's ability to see what radar cannot is especially important as distance from the radar site increases, because the radar beam becomes progressively higher in altitude further away from the radar, chiefly due to curvature of Earth, and the beam also spreads out.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-airbusradar_103-1"><a href="#cite_note-airbusradar-103">[103]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Visual_evidence">Visual evidence</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wall_cloud12_-_NOAA.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1180" data-file-width="1836" decoding="async" height="141" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Wall_cloud12_-_NOAA.jpg/220px-Wall_cloud12_-_NOAA.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Wall_cloud12_-_NOAA.jpg/330px-Wall_cloud12_-_NOAA.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Wall_cloud12_-_NOAA.jpg/440px-Wall_cloud12_-_NOAA.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wall_cloud12_-_NOAA.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A rotating <a href="/wiki/Wall_cloud" title="Wall cloud">wall cloud</a> with <a href="/wiki/Rear_flank_downdraft" title="Rear flank downdraft">rear flank downdraft</a> clear slot evident to its left rear</div></div></div>
<p>Storm spotters are trained to discern whether or not a storm seen from a distance is a supercell. They typically look to its rear, the main region of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Updraft" title="Updraft">updraft</a> and inflow. Under that updraft is a rain-free base, and the next step of <a href="/wiki/Tornadogenesis" title="Tornadogenesis">tornadogenesis</a> is the formation of a rotating <a href="/wiki/Wall_cloud" title="Wall cloud">wall cloud</a>. The vast majority of intense tornadoes occur with a wall cloud on the backside of a supercell.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Basic_Spotter_Guide_70-1"><a href="#cite_note-Basic_Spotter_Guide-70">[70]</a></sup>
</p><p>Evidence of a supercell is based on the storm's shape and structure, and cloud tower features such as a hard and vigorous updraft tower, a persistent, large <a href="/wiki/Overshooting_top" title="Overshooting top">overshooting top</a>, a hard anvil (especially when backsheared against strong upper level <a href="/wiki/Wind" title="Wind">winds</a>), and a corkscrew look or <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Striation_(meteorology)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Striation (meteorology) (page does not exist)">striations</a>. Under the storm and closer to where most tornadoes are found, evidence of a supercell and the likelihood of a tornado includes inflow bands (particularly when curved) such as a "beaver tail", and other clues such as strength of inflow, warmth and moistness of inflow air, how outflow- or inflow-dominant a storm appears, and how far is the front flank precipitation core from the wall cloud. Tornadogenesis is most likely at the interface of the updraft and <a href="/wiki/Rear_flank_downdraft" title="Rear flank downdraft">rear flank downdraft</a>, and requires a balance between the outflow and inflow.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Advanced_Spotter_Guide_18-7"><a href="#cite_note-Advanced_Spotter_Guide-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p><p>Only wall clouds that rotate spawn tornadoes, and they usually precede the tornado between five and thirty minutes. Rotating wall clouds may be a visual manifestation of a low-level mesocyclone. Barring a low-level boundary, tornadogenesis is highly unlikely unless a rear flank downdraft occurs, which is usually visibly evidenced by evaporation of cloud adjacent to a corner of a wall cloud. A tornado often occurs as this happens or shortly afterwards; first, a funnel cloud dips and in nearly all cases by the time it reaches halfway down, a surface swirl has already developed, signifying a tornado is on the ground before condensation connects the surface circulation to the storm. Tornadoes may also develop without wall clouds, under flanking lines and on the leading edge. Spotters watch all areas of a storm, and the <a href="/wiki/Cloud_base" title="Cloud base">cloud base</a> and surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NSSL_tornadoes_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-NSSL_tornadoes-112">[112]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Extremes">Extremes</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Tornado_records" title="Tornado records">Tornado records</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Super_Outbreak_Map.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1631" data-file-width="1345" decoding="async" height="267" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Super_Outbreak_Map.jpg/220px-Super_Outbreak_Map.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Super_Outbreak_Map.jpg/330px-Super_Outbreak_Map.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Super_Outbreak_Map.jpg/440px-Super_Outbreak_Map.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Super_Outbreak_Map.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A map of the tornado paths in the Super Outbreak (April 3–4, 1974)</div></div></div>
<p>The tornado which holds most records in history was the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tri-State_Tornado" title="Tri-State Tornado">Tri-State Tornado</a>, which roared through parts of <a href="/wiki/Missouri" title="Missouri">Missouri</a>, <a href="/wiki/Illinois" title="Illinois">Illinois</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Indiana" title="Indiana">Indiana</a> on March 18, 1925. It was likely an F5, though tornadoes were not ranked on any scale in that era. It holds records for longest path length (219 miles; 352 km), longest duration (about 3.5 hours), and fastest forward speed for a significant tornado (73 mph; 117 km/h) anywhere on Earth. In addition, it is the deadliest single tornado in United States history (695 dead).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-significant_tornadoes_23-9"><a href="#cite_note-significant_tornadoes-23">[23]</a></sup> The tornado was also the costliest tornado in history at the time (unadjusted for inflation), but in the years since has been surpassed by several others if population changes over time are not considered. When costs are normalized for wealth and inflation, it ranks third today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tornado_damage_cost_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-tornado_damage_cost-113">[113]</a></sup>
</p><p>The deadliest tornado in world history was the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Daultipur-Salturia_Tornado" title="Daultipur-Salturia Tornado">Daultipur-Salturia Tornado</a> in Bangladesh on April 26, 1989, which killed approximately 1,300 people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bangladesh_tornado_82-2"><a href="#cite_note-Bangladesh_tornado-82">[82]</a></sup><span class="citation-needed-content" style="padding-left:0.1em; padding-right:0.1em; color:#595959; border:1px solid #DDD;">Bangladesh has had at least 19 tornadoes in its history that killed more than 100 people, almost half of the <a href="/wiki/List_of_tornadoes_causing_100_or_more_deaths" title="List of tornadoes causing 100 or more deaths">total in the rest of the world</a>.</span><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>One of the most extensive <a href="/wiki/Tornado_outbreak" title="Tornado outbreak">tornado outbreaks</a> on record was the <a href="/wiki/1974_Super_Outbreak" title="1974 Super Outbreak">1974 Super Outbreak</a>, which affected a large area of the central United States and extreme southern <a href="/wiki/Ontario" title="Ontario">Ontario</a> on April 3 and 4, 1974. The outbreak featured 148 tornadoes in 18 hours, many of which were violent; six were of F5 intensity, and twenty-four peaked at F4 strength. Sixteen tornadoes were on the ground at the same time during its peak. More than 300 people, possibly as many as 330, were killed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-super_outbreak_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-super_outbreak-114">[114]</a></sup>
</p><p>While direct measurement of the most violent tornado wind speeds is nearly impossible, since conventional <a href="/wiki/Anemometer" title="Anemometer">anemometers</a> would be destroyed by the intense winds and flying debris, some tornadoes have been scanned by <a href="/wiki/Doppler_on_Wheels" title="Doppler on Wheels">mobile Doppler radar units</a>, which can provide a good estimate of the tornado's winds. The highest wind speed ever measured in a tornado, which is also the highest wind speed ever recorded on the planet, is 301 ± 20 mph (484 ± 32 km/h) in the F5 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1999_Bridge_Creek_%E2%80%93_Moore_tornado" title="1999 Bridge Creek – Moore tornado">Bridge Creek-Moore, Oklahoma</a>, tornado which killed 36 people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> The reading was taken about 100 feet (30 m) above the ground.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fastest_wind_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-fastest_wind-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p><p>Storms that produce tornadoes can feature intense updrafts, sometimes exceeding 150 mph (240 km/h). Debris from a tornado can be lofted into the parent storm and carried a very long distance. A tornado which affected <a href="/wiki/Great_Bend,_Kansas" title="Great Bend, Kansas">Great Bend, Kansas</a>, in November 1915, was an extreme case, where a "rain of debris" occurred 80 miles (130 km) from the town, a sack of flour was found 110 miles (180 km) away, and a cancelled check from the Great Bend bank was found in a field outside of <a href="/wiki/Palmyra,_Nebraska" title="Palmyra, Nebraska">Palmyra, Nebraska</a>, 305 miles (491 km) to the northeast.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tornado_project_oddities_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-tornado_project_oddities-116">[116]</a></sup> Waterspouts and tornadoes have been advanced as an explanation for instances of <a href="/wiki/Rain_of_animals" title="Rain of animals">raining fish and other animals</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Safety">Safety</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Tornado_preparedness" title="Tornado preparedness">Tornado preparedness</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Birmingham_tornado_2005_damage.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Birmingham_tornado_2005_damage.jpg/220px-Birmingham_tornado_2005_damage.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Birmingham_tornado_2005_damage.jpg/330px-Birmingham_tornado_2005_damage.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Birmingham_tornado_2005_damage.jpg/440px-Birmingham_tornado_2005_damage.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Birmingham_tornado_2005_damage.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Damage from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Birmingham_tornado_of_2005" title="Birmingham tornado of 2005">Birmingham tornado of 2005</a>. An unusually strong example of a tornado event in the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a>, the Birmingham Tornado resulted in 19 injuries, mostly from falling trees.</div></div></div>
<p>Though tornadoes can strike in an instant, there are precautions and preventative measures that can be taken to increase the chances of survival. Authorities such as the <a href="/wiki/Storm_Prediction_Center" title="Storm Prediction Center">Storm Prediction Center</a> in the United States advise having a pre-determined plan should a tornado warning be issued. When a warning is issued, going to a basement or an interior first-floor room of a sturdy building greatly increases chances of survival.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tornado_safety_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-tornado_safety-118">[118]</a></sup> In tornado-prone areas, many buildings have underground <a href="/wiki/Storm_cellar" title="Storm cellar">storm cellars</a>, which have saved thousands of lives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some countries have meteorological agencies which distribute tornado forecasts and increase levels of alert of a possible tornado (such as <a href="/wiki/Tornado_watch" title="Tornado watch">tornado watches</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tornado_warning" title="Tornado warning">warnings</a> in the United States and Canada). <a href="/wiki/Weather_radio" title="Weather radio">Weather radios</a> provide an alarm when a severe weather advisory is issued for the local area, mainly available only in the United States. Unless the tornado is far away and highly visible, meteorologists advise that drivers park their vehicles far to the side of the road (so as not to block emergency traffic), and find a sturdy shelter. If no sturdy shelter is nearby, getting low in a ditch is the next best option. Highway overpasses are one of the worst places to take shelter during tornadoes, as the constricted space can be subject to increased wind speed and funneling of debris underneath the overpass.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-highway_overpasses_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-highway_overpasses-120">[120]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Myths_and_misconceptions">Myths and misconceptions</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Tornado_myths" title="Tornado myths">Tornado myths</a></div>
<p>Folklore often identifies a green sky with tornadoes, and though the phenomenon may be associated with severe weather, there is no evidence linking it specifically with tornadoes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup> It is often thought that opening windows will lessen the damage caused by the tornado. While there is a large drop in <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_pressure" title="Atmospheric pressure">atmospheric pressure</a> inside a strong tornado, the pressure difference is unlikely to cause significant damage. Opening windows may instead increase the severity of the tornado's damage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tornado_project_myths_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-tornado_project_myths-122">[122]</a></sup> A violent tornado can destroy a house whether its windows are open or closed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tornado_project_myths_122-1"><a href="#cite_note-tornado_project_myths-122">[122]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tornado_myths_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-tornado_myths-123">[123]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1999_Salt_Lake_City_tornado.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="242" data-file-width="320" decoding="async" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/1999_Salt_Lake_City_tornado.jpg/220px-1999_Salt_Lake_City_tornado.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/3e/1999_Salt_Lake_City_tornado.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:1999_Salt_Lake_City_tornado.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/1999_Salt_Lake_City_tornado" title="1999 Salt Lake City tornado">1999 Salt Lake City tornado</a> disproved several misconceptions, including the idea that tornadoes cannot occur in cities.</div></div></div>
<p>Another commonly held misconception is that highway overpasses provide adequate shelter from tornadoes. This belief is partly inspired by widely circulated video captured during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/April_26,_1991_tornado_outbreak" title="April 26, 1991 tornado outbreak">1991 tornado outbreak</a> near <a href="/wiki/Andover,_Kansas" title="Andover, Kansas">Andover, Kansas</a>, where a news crew and several other people took shelter under an overpass on the <a href="/wiki/Kansas_Turnpike" title="Kansas Turnpike">Kansas Turnpike</a> and safely rode out a tornado as it passed by.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-noaa-overpass_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-noaa-overpass-124">[124]</a></sup> However, a highway overpass is a dangerous place during a tornado, and the subjects of the video remained safe due to an unlikely combination of events: the storm in question was a weak tornado, the tornado did not directly strike the overpass, and the overpass itself was of a unique design.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-noaa-overpass_124-1"><a href="#cite_note-noaa-overpass-124">[124]</a></sup> Due to the <a href="/wiki/Venturi_effect" title="Venturi effect">Venturi effect</a>, tornadic winds are accelerated in the confined space of an overpass.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup> Indeed, in the <a href="/wiki/1999_Oklahoma_tornado_outbreak" title="1999 Oklahoma tornado outbreak">1999 Oklahoma tornado outbreak</a> of May 3, 1999, three highway overpasses were directly struck by tornadoes, and at each of the three locations there was a fatality, along with many life-threatening injuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-highway_overpass_danger_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-highway_overpass_danger-126">[126]</a></sup> By comparison, during the same tornado outbreak, more than 2,000 homes were completely destroyed and another 7,000 damaged, and yet only a few dozen people died in their homes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-highway_overpasses_120-1"><a href="#cite_note-highway_overpasses-120">[120]</a></sup>
</p><p>An old belief is that the southwest corner of a basement provides the most protection during a tornado. The safest place is the side or corner of an underground room opposite the tornado's direction of approach (usually the northeast corner), or the central-most room on the lowest floor. Taking shelter in a basement, under a staircase, or under a sturdy piece of furniture such as a workbench further increases the chances of survival.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tornado_project_myths_122-2"><a href="#cite_note-tornado_project_myths-122">[122]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tornado_myths_123-1"><a href="#cite_note-tornado_myths-123">[123]</a></sup>
</p><p>There are areas which people believe to be protected from tornadoes, whether by being in a city, near a major river, hill, or mountain, or even protected by <a href="/wiki/Supernatural" title="Supernatural">supernatural</a> forces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup> Tornadoes have been known to cross major rivers, climb mountains,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tornadoes_in_mountains_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tornadoes_in_mountains-128">[128]</a></sup> affect valleys, and have damaged <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_tornadoes_striking_downtown_areas" title="List of tornadoes striking downtown areas">several city centers</a>. As a general rule, no area is safe from tornadoes, though some areas are more susceptible than others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Handy_Weather_Answer_Book_25-6"><a href="#cite_note-Handy_Weather_Answer_Book-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tornado_project_myths_122-3"><a href="#cite_note-tornado_project_myths-122">[122]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tornado_myths_123-2"><a href="#cite_note-tornado_myths-123">[123]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ongoing_research">Ongoing research</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tornado_with_DOW.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1116" data-file-width="1552" decoding="async" height="158" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Tornado_with_DOW.jpg/220px-Tornado_with_DOW.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Tornado_with_DOW.jpg/330px-Tornado_with_DOW.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Tornado_with_DOW.jpg/440px-Tornado_with_DOW.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tornado_with_DOW.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Doppler_on_Wheels" title="Doppler on Wheels">Doppler on Wheels</a> unit observing a tornado near <a href="/wiki/Attica,_Kansas" title="Attica, Kansas">Attica, Kansas</a></div></div></div>
<p>Meteorology is a relatively young science and the study of tornadoes is newer still. Although researched for about 140 years and intensively for around 60 years, there are still aspects of tornadoes which remain a mystery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VORTEX_book_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-VORTEX_book-129">[129]</a></sup> Meteorologists have a fairly good understanding of the development of <a href="/wiki/Thunderstorm" title="Thunderstorm">thunderstorms</a> and mesocyclones,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Extreme_Weather_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-Extreme_Weather-130">[130]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup> and the meteorological conditions conducive to their formation. However, the step from <a href="/wiki/Supercell" title="Supercell">supercell</a>, or other respective formative processes, to <a href="/wiki/Tornadogenesis" title="Tornadogenesis">tornadogenesis</a> and the prediction of tornadic vs. non-tornadic mesocyclones is not yet well known and is the focus of much research.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LouieLouie_85-1"><a href="#cite_note-LouieLouie-85">[85]</a></sup>
</p><p>Also under study are the low-level mesocyclone and the <a href="/wiki/Vortex_stretching" title="Vortex stretching">stretching</a> of low-level <a href="/wiki/Vorticity" title="Vorticity">vorticity</a> which tightens into a tornado,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LouieLouie_85-2"><a href="#cite_note-LouieLouie-85">[85]</a></sup> in particular, what are the processes and what is the relationship of the environment and the convective storm. Intense tornadoes have been observed forming simultaneously with a mesocyclone aloft (rather than succeeding mesocyclogenesis) and some intense tornadoes have occurred without a mid-level mesocyclone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Seymour_2001_32_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-Seymour_2001_32-132">[132]</a></sup>
</p><p>In particular, the role of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Downdraft" title="Downdraft">downdrafts</a>, particularly the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rear-flank_downdraft" title="Rear-flank downdraft">rear-flank downdraft</a>, and the role of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baroclinic" title="Baroclinic">baroclinic</a> boundaries, are intense areas of study.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup>
</p><p>Reliably predicting tornado intensity and longevity remains a problem, as do details affecting characteristics of a tornado during its life cycle and tornadolysis. Other rich areas of research are tornadoes associated with <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/mesovortex" title="wikt:mesovortex">mesovortices</a> within linear thunderstorm structures and within tropical cyclones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tornado_forecasting_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-tornado_forecasting-134">[134]</a></sup>
</p><p>Meteorologists still do not know the exact mechanisms by which most tornadoes form, and occasional tornadoes still strike without a tornado warning being issued.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup> Analysis of observations including both stationary and mobile (surface and aerial) <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/In-situ" title="In-situ">in-situ</a> and <a href="/wiki/Remote_sensing" title="Remote sensing">remote sensing</a> (passive and active) instruments generates new ideas and refines existing notions. <a href="/wiki/Mathematical_model" title="Mathematical model">Numerical modeling</a> also provides new insights as observations and new discoveries are integrated into our physical understanding and then tested in <a href="/wiki/Computer_simulation" title="Computer simulation">computer simulations</a> which validate new notions as well as produce entirely new theoretical findings, many of which are otherwise unattainable. Importantly, development of new observation technologies and installation of finer spatial and temporal resolution observation networks have aided increased understanding and better predictions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup>
</p><p>Research programs, including field projects such as the <a href="/wiki/VORTEX_projects" title="VORTEX projects">VORTEX projects</a> (Verification of the Origins of Rotation in Tornadoes Experiment), deployment of <a href="/wiki/TOtable_Tornado_Observatory" title="TOtable Tornado Observatory">TOTO</a> (the TOtable Tornado Observatory), Doppler on Wheels (DOW), and dozens of other programs, hope to solve many questions that still plague meteorologists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-field_programs_history_46-2"><a href="#cite_note-field_programs_history-46">[46]</a></sup> Universities, government agencies such as the <a href="/wiki/National_Severe_Storms_Laboratory" title="National Severe Storms Laboratory">National Severe Storms Laboratory</a>, private-sector meteorologists, and the <a href="/wiki/National_Center_for_Atmospheric_Research" title="National Center for Atmospheric Research">National Center for Atmospheric Research</a> are some of the organizations very active in research; with various sources of funding, both private and public, a chief entity being the <a href="/wiki/National_Science_Foundation" title="National Science Foundation">National Science Foundation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NSSLsearch_110-1"><a href="#cite_note-NSSLsearch-110">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup> The pace of research is partly constrained by the number of observations that can be taken; gaps in information about the wind, pressure, and moisture content throughout the local atmosphere; and the computing power available for simulation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup>
</p><p>Solar storms similar to tornadoes have been recorded, but it is unknown how closely related they are to their terrestrial counterparts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup>
</p>
